\_sh v3.0  1281 Text

\id beseda_0000_metadata
\ref Recorded on 18 February 2010 in Sebjan-Küöl
by Brigitte Pakendorf and Natalia Aralova in the
house rented by NA and BP. All four speakers
work at the village school. They were invited for
tea and were asked to talk with each other. NA
and BP prepared some tea and cookies, turned
on the camera and audio recorder and left the
room. The women appeared to have forgotten
about the recording devices, so that the
conversation is quite animated - though it later
transpired they were well aware throughout that
they were being recorded.
\qu Transcribed with pen and paper by Ija Vasil'evna
Krivoshapkina, transcribed into ELAN by NA and
BP (half:half); translated into Russian by IVK. The
first quarter was glossed by NA in 2010; the latter
three quarters were glossed by BP in 2012. Funding
from the Volkswagen Foundation (DoBeS project).
Speaker abbreviations = RDA, NPZ, IAS, LNZ
\ref Raisa Dmitrievna Alekseeva (RDA) was born 10
Feb 1961, so she was 49 at the time of the
recording. She holds a university degree and was
working in the school as psychologist at the time
of the recording; every summer, however, she
went to the reindeer herd as a "tent worker"
(chumrabotnitsa). She is the sister of Innokentij
Dmitrievich Burtsev. 
\ref Lilija Nikolaevna Zaxarova (LNZ) was born in 1970, so she was 39-40 years old at the time of recording. She has a university degree and works as a teacher of
Russian language and literature at the school; doesn't normally speak much Even, rather speaks Sakha (Yakut). Is the sister of Vasilij Nikolaevich Zaxarov.

\id beseda_0017
\tx Ile           ịhaččaːdʒịl?            Koːltirre       ịsčadʒị(l)?
\mb irek  -(dU)LE ịs  -E-Č   -čE    -DʒI -L  Koːltir -(dU)LE ịs  -čE    -DʒI    -L
\ge which -loc    reach -ep-res -pf.ptc -fut  -pl Koltir   -loc    reach -pf.ptc -fut -pl
\ft Where will they come? Will they come to Koltir?
\ph
\gr ịhaččaːdʒil, ịsčadʒị(l) -- plural marker -l doesn't fit here, but according to the translator, in the family of the speaker it is used instead of -r (that
would be a corret form). -ča-dʒịl means here a hypothetical action, probably it is used here because of the question (Ija corrected her translation,
adding li). isaddaj has more imperfective meaning than istaj. 
\nt Koltir is a place in the route of the nineth herd, it will be discussed further..
\ru  Куда добираются ли? В Колтир добрались ли?
\ELANBegin 00:01:13.982
\ELANEnd 00:01:17.742

\id beseda_0048
\tx GUPs...                     GUP                        jegeren              herrin.
\mb GUP                         GUP                        jeger   -E-n(I)      hor -RI       -n(I)
\ge state.unitary.enterprise.R  state.unitary.enterprise.R jeger.R -ep-poss.3sg go  -impf.ptc -poss.3sg

\ft The "jeger" of the GUP (state unitary enterprise) has left.
\ph
\gr
\nt GUP (gosudarstvennoe unitarnoe predprijatie) is the organization in Sebjan that owns all reindeer herds, that manages all reindeer breeding in
Sebjan Küöl. A "jeger" is a type of truck that has space for cargo at the back but also has a fairly big cabin with place for more than just the driver
and one or two passengers, so being able to take several passengers at once
\ru ГУПовский егерь уехал
\ELANBegin 00:01:57.500
\ELANEnd 00:01:59.054
\ELANParticipant RDA

\id beseda_0054
\tx Ičukenemi                  herre
\mb it  -WkEn -nE     -mI       hor -R(E)
\ge see -caus -intent -cond.cvb go  -nonfut

\ft They went to be seen (to see a doctor)
\ph
\gr when I asked on 050312, it seemed as if ičukenemi = pojti pokazatjsa (i.e. it should be with the intentional here), and just "pokazatjsja" = ičukemi;
but IVK just mm-ed in answer to my suggestions, so I'm not really sure I got it right and I would like to check that again;
\gr on 250312 I checked this again: ičukenemi = esli pokazat'sja vraču; ja sižu pokazyvaja knigu = ịcụkeniken; kogda ja pokazyvala knigu ja uronila
ee - NOT ičukenemi; ičukenemi = poexali pokazyvajasja; ičukemi = pokazyvaja, just the normal showing, while ičukenemi has the meaning of
"pokazatsja vraču"

\id beseda_0546
\tx ŋa... hịːralča              bihin
\mb        hịːr  -E-L     -čE    bi -RI -n(I)
\ge        be.angry -ep-inch -pf.ptc be -pst -poss.3sg

\ft s/he got angry
\ph
\gr just "hịralča" - perfective, the result is visible now, the person is angry now, not clear why, the speaker only sees the result, not the process, the
cause of the anger; hịralča bihin = he was angry then, in the past; ja togda rasserdilas' = hịrallịw; hịralča bihiw = pragmatically odd, because it's as
if the speaker doesn't know that she was angry; hịrallịn = OK, here the anger was witnessed by the speaker, the whole process of the person getting
angry, while hịralča bihin the speaker is not a witness, or it wasn't because of the speaker, but it seems as if the distant past meaning is dominant
(though it can also depend on the context) (all this elicited during translation session, file beseda_240212_transl_500_570, 28:30-30:30)
\ru она рассердилась (он рассердился)

\id beseda_0562
\tx Heːdʒennin                 ịbga   ọːngol.
\mb heːdʒe -n -RI -n(I)        ịbgọ ọːn =(W)Ul
\ge dance -nr -ipf.ptc -poss.3sg good     how    =indef

\ft She dances well (her dancing is good).
\ph
\gr the word order (verb followed by adjective) and the fact that ịbga is in the adjectival and not adverbial form (ịbgat) indicates that heːdʒennin is not
the finite past tense form of the verb 'to dance', but the participial form, 'her dancing'; 'she danced well' = ịbgat heːdʒennin; ịbgat hedʒennin = at
that moment in the past she danced well, while heːdʒennin ịbga = in general she dances well, a general property, very close or synonymous in
meaning is ịbgat heːdʒengeren 
\nt
\ru Хорошо она танцует (его танцевание хорошее)

\id beseda_0629
\tx Ịak hụtaːkịn                bidni                 ehnittu              gerbette

\ft how greedily she eats, probably (hopefully) she doesn't think that
\ph
\gr the bidni = wrong form, the correct nonfuture form is either bihni or bidden (with progressive), and it is also syntactically wrong here, it should
correctly just be ịak hụtaːkịn ehnittu gerbette, without the bidni; ehnittu gerbette = naverno tak ne dumaet (with element of hope, I hope she
doesn't think so); ịak hụtaːkịn - said about herself, "čto za obžora" - she's using the 3rd person here because she's taking our perspective "they will
probably not think (I hope) how greedy she is"; one can also say ịak hụtaːkịw and ịak hụtaːkịs --> so the poss.3sg cannot be referring to ịak!
\nt LNZ says about someone that she is eating too greedily and says that we probably don't think so
\gr 260312: hụtaːkịn is derived from a verb hụtaːdaj "obzhiratsja", but no adjective *hụtaːkụ exists
\ru какая она жадно кушает, она наверно так не думает

\ft and I came without having closed my stove
\ph
\gr emče bihem = here used, because when she left the house she didn't remember, she only remembers now; if she had said "ọhọkịda eniken dahra
emnem" it would mean that she didn't close the flue on purpose, that she didn't want to close the flue; but in this case she didn't do it intentionally,
she just forgot; "eniken dahra emniw" would have been in the past (i.e. an event that took place in the past but is already over) - but since she's still
sitting there, she uses the perfective participle

the heat escapes quickly and the house can cool off

\id beseda_1071
\tx mute   majrịdʒị      atta     ịala     emgere
\mb mut    maj       -RIdʒI  ač =DE  ịak -LE  em   -Gr(E)
\ge 1pl.in ruin.luck -ant.cvb neg =ptl what  -prv come -hab

\ft we ruined their luck (lit. our luck was ruined), and they come without anything
\ph
\gr probably meant to say mut majụkanịdʒụr = we having ruined their luck; maj- = to spoil someone's desire to do something, with regard to hunting
it is to spoil the hunting spirits willingness/desire to give prey; majča = the spirit of the earth/spirit of hunting has changed its mind and doesn't give
prey, and this happens because people have talked beforehand about what kind of prey there'll be, about what they'll be eating; the correct way to
express what she wanted to say would be: mut majụkanakat, atta ịala emgerer = when we jinx them, they come back without anything; majran
(used without subject) = the spirit of the earth lost interest; but can also be said of a person, majran = someone wanted to do something but then
changed their mind because someone else spoiled their desire/interest to do it; (quest_beseda_977-1333, 18:15-20:15) majran = when you're talking
about the hunting spirit, then you just say "majran" without subject; when you're talking about having spoiled someone's desire to do something,
then you do say the subject (though it can presumably be dropped like all subjects), biː majram = ja sobiralasj idti na sljot, but someone spoiled my
wish to go, so biː majram, hiː majrannị are fine
\gr emgere - a frequently used variant of emgerer
\nt
\ru мы повергнувшись сглазу, они без ничего не приходят

\id beseda_1153
\tx Upeːńdʒe...      abagańdʒa         ịadaŋan                    goːli,        maːdaŋan.
\mb upeː       -ńdʒE abaga       -ńdʒE ịa    -DEŋ    -E-n(I)      goːn -LI      maː -DEŋ    -E-n(I)
\ge grandmother -aug    grandfather -aug    do.what -pst.ptc -ep-poss.3sg say   -imp.2sg kill -pst.ptc -ep-poss.3sg
\ft the grandmother... the grandfather did what, killed
\ph
\gr it would be grammatically more correct to have the ablative case on maːdaŋdụkụn, but without the case-marking it's fully OK, maybe even better
than with the case-marking (that would be "too long"); and even though upeːńdʒe and abagańdʒa refer to Marisha's grandparents, it's fully OK to
have them without possessive marking here; however, the person-marking on the verb is sg because it refers only to the grandfather, it was him
who killed the tarbagans that went into the fur coat, and she by mistake said "upeːńdʒe", then corrected that to "abagańdʒa"; if if had been both who
killed the tarbagans, it would have to be maːdaŋatnan
\gr (quest_beseda_977-1333, 27:15-29:45) checked with her, if it had been "vyshla shuba iz tex tarbaganov kotorye ubil ded", then one would say abaga
maːdaŋdụkụn; here, the complete sentence would be "abagańdʒa maːdaŋan čaːmaktụk" 'from the tarbagans which the ded had killed'; and it's fine to
have no possessive marking on the grandparents because of the augmentative suffix, one wouldn't say just "abaga" to refer to somebody's
grandfather, but "abagan"
\ru бабушка, дедушка что-то сделал, убил (то что убил дедушка)
\ELANBegin 00:33:37.495
\ELANEnd 00:33:41.730

\ph
\gr ehes bakamha = lit. "don't you want to find me", but here has a meaning of "can't you find"; ühühni = fine, "govorjat zhe, vedj"
\nt after the story about the ghost who wasn't one, they are returning to the beginning of the ghost stories, namely to the man on the snowmobile
(1167-1190); a man (the head of the neighbouring Segen-Kuel village) had crashed his snowmobile in a river, and he either drowned or got
dragged off by a bear (cf. 1267); the snowmobile was found, but not the body, and here the idea is that his spirit or soul is still hanging around the
place of the accident, and is visiting people in their dreams, asking them to find the body
\ru как вы меня не можете найти, говоря, куда-то к ним (в их куда-то - в их сны) приходил же говорят ночью

\id beseda_1383
\tx kụŋańdʒa
\mb kụŋa -ńdʒE
\ge child   -aug
\ft my child
\gr it's OK to say kụŋańdʒa about one's own child, without the possessive - but not without the augmentative, you don't say "kụŋa" when you are
talking about your own child, then you say kụŋawụ, though you can say kụŋańdʒa, like here, though kụŋańdʒaw, with the possessive, is also fine;
they use terms of relationship with the augmentative and without possessives when it is clear who they are talking about - i.e. there was a reference
to upeːńdʒe, abagańdʒa (1153) which didn't refer to the speakers grandparents, but it was clear that she was referring to the grandparents of the
little girl who had lost her fur jacket

\nt LNZ has an iron stove at home, not a brick stove. These iron stoves need to be fired continuously to keep the house warm, because as soon as the
fire dies down,the stove cools off and  so does the house. (In contrast, brick stoves retain the heat for a long time, so that it is sufficient to heat once
or twice a day, usually mornings and evenings, three times in very cold weather.) This means that people with iron stoves need a lot more firewood
than those with brick stoves (and even those need a lot of wood, about four truckloads full for a household with a brick stove to survive the
winter). Since LNZ is a single mother, she has no man to provide her with the much-needed firewood and therefore has to resort to such demeaning
habits as going to ask others for wood (1431-1499) or picking it up off the street.
